class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/98078520\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078521 id=\"98078521\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>6 CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Given the sky coverage, depth and excellent resolution of <em>Gaia</em>'s first data release, it is a prime data set to use for lensed quasar searches. Starting from a quasar candidate catalogue, the <em>Gaia</em> source catalogue can be searched for multiple detections for each candidate, quickly identifying likely lensed quasars. This method will identify \u223c900 lensed quasars (\u223c240 quads) with image separations above 0.5 arcsec across the whole sky (Oguri &amp; Marshall\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Finet &amp; Surdej\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). We perform this search in a variety of photometric quasar catalogues and present the results in a companion paper (Lemon et\u00a0al., in preparation).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">However, GDR1 has often catalogued only one component of lensed quasars, in particular for systems with small image separations (Arenou et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). It is at these separations that we would benefit most from <em>Gaia</em>, since ground-based imaging is unable to resolve the separate images of a lensed quasar. Therefore we have developed a method to exploit a single <em>Gaia</em> detection to find the population of small-separation lensed quasars. This method relies on <em>Gaia</em> effectively resolving out the flux from just one image of a lensed quasar, whereas ground-based observations (from SDSS and Pan-STARRS) blend the components together. For a sample of 49 known small-separation lensed quasars, we demonstrate that the Pan-STARRS flux is larger than the <em>Gaia</em> flux, verifying the idea that <em>Gaia</em> is only measuring a single image. We also show that the offset between <em>Gaia</em> and SDSS positions for our sample of lensed quasars is significant, because the <em>Gaia</em> centroid lies on top of the detected image, whereas in SDSS it is at the luminosity-weighted centroid of the system.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">By performing the same flux and centroid difference measurements on spectroscopically confirmed SDSS quasars, we are able to search for new lensed quasars. Inspecting better-seeing data of the systems with the largest flux and centroid offsets, we find four new sub-arcsecond lensed quasar candidates with resolved components in HSC or CFHT data. At such small separations, projected systems (e.g. quasar+star) are less common, and so lensed quasars selected in this way are less contaminated.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As future <em>Gaia</em> data releases improve the completeness of secondary source detection in close pairs, multiple <em>Gaia</em> detections will become an easily-implemented method to find lensed quasar candidates. However, our method of using single <em>Gaia</em> detections will still be a useful tool to discover lensed quasars that have only one image bright enough to be detected by <em>Gaia</em>. This will include \u223c1400 lensed quasars. Furthermore, as wide-field optical surveys become deeper, the centroid offset will become a more robust statistic for differentiating between single quasars and lensed quasars. The task will then be to remove contaminant systems such as quasar and star alignments. <em>Gaia</em>'s long temporal baseline and repeated measurements will help select systems with similar component variability and its blue and red photometer instruments can ensure components have a similar colour.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Finally, we note that these methods are not restricted to lensed quasar searches, but should be useful for searches for stellar binary companions, or to remove contaminants from samples of isolated quasars. These techniques demonstrate how <em>Gaia</em>'s excellent resolution provides an important complement to future deep ground-based optical surveys.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078527 id=\"98078527\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgements</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We thank Diana Harrison and Lindsay Oldham for useful discussions and comments about the paper. CAL would like to thank the Science and Technology Facilities Council (STFC) for their studentship. MWA also acknowledges support from the STFC in the form of an Ernest Rutherford Fellowship. This paper includes data based on observations obtained at the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope (CFHT) which is operated by the National Research Council of Canada, the Institut National des Sciences de l'Univers of the Centre National de la Recherche Scientifique of France and the University of Hawaii.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">The best-fitting formulae using SDSS photometry are: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"\" content-id=\"\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray*}\r\n {g-r} &lt; 0.24: {G} \\!=\\! 0.029 \\!+\\! {r} \\!+\\! 0.139 ({g-r}) \\!-\\! 0.641 ({r-i}) \\!-\\! 0.496 ({i-z})\\\\\r\n {g-r} &gt; 0.24: {G} \\!=\\! \\!-\\!0.038 \\!+\\! {r} \\!+\\! 0.138 ({g-r}) \\!-\\! 0.400 ({r-i}) \\!-\\! 0.163 ({i-z})\r\n\\end{eqnarray*} </div></div></div></p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078531 id=\"98078531\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1\" data-legacy-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib1\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib1\"></a></span></div><div class=\"ref false\"><div id=\"ref-auto-bib1\" class=\"ref-content \"><div class=\"mixed-citation citation\"><p class=\"mixed-citation-compatibility\">\r\n<span class=\"person-group\"><span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Agnello</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">A.</div></span> <span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Grillo</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">C.</div></span> <span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Jones</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">T.</div></span> <span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Treu</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">T.</div></span> <span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Bonamigo</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">M.</div></span> <span class=\"name string-name\"><div class=\"surname\">Suyu</div>  <div class=\"given-names\">S. class=\"site-menu-item site-menu-lvl-0\">\r\n                        <a href=\"javascript:;\" class=\"nav-link mobile-account-institution dummy-link\">\r\n                            University of Utah\r\n                        </a>\r\n\r\n                    <i class=\"mobile-nav-arrow icon-general_arrow-down\"></i>\r\n                    <ul class=\"site-menu site-menu-lvl-1\">\r\n                            <li class=\"dropdown-institution-item\">\r\n\r\nUniversity of Utah\r\n                            </li>\r\n\r\n                        <li class=\"dropdown-institution-signout\">\r\n href=\"/my-account/register?siteId=5326&amp;returnUrl=%2fmnras%2farticle%2f472%2f4%2f5023%2f4083624\" class=\"register nav-link\">Register</a>\r\n                </li>\r\n        </ul>\r\n <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078446 id=\"98078446\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em>'s exceptional resolution (FWHM\u00a0\u223c\u00a00.1 arcsec) allows identification and cataloguing of the multiple images of gravitationally lensed quasars. We investigate a sample of 49 known lensed quasars in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) footprint, with image separations less than 2 arcsec, and find that eight are detected with multiple components in the first <em>Gaia</em> data release. In the case of the 41 single <em>Gaia</em> detections, we generally are able to distinguish these lensed quasars from single quasars when comparing <em>Gaia</em> flux and position measurements to those of Pan-STARRS and SDSS. This is because the multiple images of these lensed quasars are typically blended in ground-based imaging and therefore the total flux and a flux-weighted centroid are measured, which can differ significantly from the fluxes and centroids of the individual components detected by <em>Gaia</em>. We compare the fluxes through an empirical fit of Pan-STARRS <em>griz</em> photometry to the wide optical <em>Gaia</em> bandpass values using a sample of isolated quasars. The positional offsets are calculated from a recalibrated astrometric SDSS catalogue. Applying flux and centroid difference criteria to spectroscopically confirmed quasars, we discover four new sub-arcsecond-separation lensed quasar candidates which have two distinct components of similar colour in archival Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope or Hyper Suprime Cam data. Our method based on single <em>Gaia</em> detections can be used to identify the \u223c1400 lensed quasars with image separation above 0.5 arcsec, expected to have only one image bright enough to be detected by <em>Gaia</em>.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;techniques: miscellaneous&quot;\">techniques: miscellaneous</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;quasars: general&quot;\">quasars: general</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078448 id=\"98078448\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Gravitationally lensed quasars are powerful tools to study extragalactic and cosmological phenomena. As with all lenses, the unique geometry can be used for detailed study of both the sources (e.g. Ding et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Stacey et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and also the lensing galaxies (e.g. Oguri, Rusu &amp; Falco\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib47\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib47\" data-open=\"bib47\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Birrer, Amara &amp; Refregier\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Since quasars are variable sources, the time delays between the light curves of the individual lensed images can be measured (e.g. Eulaers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). These time delays depend on the different path lengths from source to observer and the gravitational potential along these paths and thus can be used to determine the Hubble constant (Refsdal\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1966</a>), recently to better than 4 percent precision (Bonvin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Each light curve is imprinted with further variations due to microlensing by stars in the lensing galaxy, making it possible to probe the structure of the source quasar accretion disc (e.g. Bate et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Motta et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and dark matter fractions in the lensing galaxy (Bate et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Schechter et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Jim\u00e9nez-Vicente et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). Though more than 150 lensed quasars are known to date, only a subset can be used to enable the above science. For example, microlensing events may be most noticeable in systems with low-redshift lenses, leading to smaller microlensing time-scales (e.g. Mosquera &amp; Kochanek\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The methods developed in this paper are aimed at finding small-separation lensed quasars, with image separations typically less than 1 arcsec. These lenses, though more difficult to find, are more common than wider-separation systems and can be used to probe either higher-redshift/lower-mass lensing galaxies and to understand mass distributions in the cores of galaxies (e.g. Trott &amp; Webster\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>). Furthermore, forming a complete sample of lensed quasars at lower separations will yield better constraints on the cosmological mass density parameter and evolution of the lensing galaxy population (Oguri et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Finet &amp; Surdej\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Dedicated follow-up programmes to determine statistical samples of lensed quasars have been based on radio surveys (e.g. Cosmic Lens All-Sky Survey (CLASS); Myers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1995</a>) or wide-field optical surveys (e.g. Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) Quasar Lens Search (SQLS); Oguri et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). Each survey has its own advantages and disadvantages; radio surveys like CLASS were only able to target radio-loud lensed sources but discovered smaller image separation objects, and SQLS had multiband optical imaging of a large fraction of the sky, but was incomplete towards the smallest-separation lenses due to the median seeing of 1.4 arcsec in SDSS. Accordingly, the median image separation of the CLASS and SQLS lenses are 0.8 and 1.65 arcsec, respectively.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Many techniques have been suggested in the literature for finding such rare systems in these vast data sets. Each method is tailored to different types of data, e.g. multiband colour classification to find quasar-like systems which are extended or deblended into multiple components (Ostrovski et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Other techniques rely on the time domain to reveal neighbouring variable objects (Kochanek et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>) or search for time delays through cross-correlations (Pindor\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>). However, many current wide-field surveys lack the necessary number of epochs to apply these methods. In many cases, the lenses must be found through single-epoch imaging and in the case of some searches start from spectroscopically confirmed quasars.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">With the advent of many wide-field optical surveys covering the whole sky, Oguri &amp; Marshall (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>, hereafter <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">OM10</a>) predicted lensed quasar numbers expected in such surveys. Crucially, these numbers rely on all lensed quasars being detected at separations above two-thirds of the point spread function (PSF) full width half-maximum (FWHM) down to the limiting magnitudes of the survey. This is a difficult task without many epochs of imaging, and so further techniques must be developed to meet this goal. It is important to note that, in agreement with these predictions, data sets like SDSS are not depleted of bright lensed quasars since searches like SQLS are biased towards certain source redshifts and quasar selection criteria (Richards et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>). SQLS also mainly targeted the candidate systems with image separation \u22731 arcsec. Indeed lensed quasars are being found in SDSS photometric data alone (Ostrovski et\u00a0al., in preparation; Agnello et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) without spectroscopic selection.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we show that the recent <em>Gaia</em> data release (Lindegren et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) is a very useful tool to overcome the difficulties of finding lensed quasars in ground-based surveys alone, based mainly on its excellent angular resolution and full-sky coverage. Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> demonstrates how <em>Gaia</em> is able to detect three of the four quasar images of PG1115+080 (Weymann et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1980</a>). The methods we develop are useful to select any optical binary. This includes stellar binaries which are key to understanding star formation and evolutionary models (e.g. Chabrier\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>; Liu, Dupuy &amp; Ireland\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) and also quasar pairs, including binary quasars and projected pairs (e.g. Hennawi et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). The brightest close-separation binary companions are often found in ground-based imaging data, using similar methods to quasar lens searches, such as looking for extended systems inconsistent with a single PSF (Deacon et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). However, this is an incomplete method at small separations, with pipeline PSF models unknowingly using these binaries to determine the local PSF, thus affecting the PSF magnitudes.</p>                        <a id=\"98078456\" scrollto-destination=\"98078456\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=sVM21RcIuvQvcH27BkObRmyEx2JlEQnN9Asww-6KnFMdgl~RNXLk1YdV8RvferFN6V74pR0e17zy0zEfjABRaH7qh-0xvgTS0djTAVmjeqP6hgCaSgrZE~4ox48OdBrhQdP33nfUygIirKIQaSvwX-Xm5PabTlEfBC1Ezd6Ju3OFKcicgxf4URP-Na4ABgH3924MxPKxC7oCqofbi9NkFGmbJ4BJt9s~AGxs6g9IpVhp8L8rk6p-N8GfAQFaDh1-SCoG-XQs2I~eLjfKA20OSGRvKn0jmwqqvXSr37P89S-7Hs2~fJUKwDzKsV-cSisFoZpR30URgRxSknb1vYSj2A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"PG1115+080 as seen in Hubble Space Telescope (left) and Pan-STARRS (right). Gaia detections are marked with white crosses.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig1.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/98078456/stx2094fig1.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig1.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"98078456\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/stx2094fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&Signature=G3ybxYu4ugCxIAzrLPcseIR7XwUYPIVrZjcIy72S8Lzg99RyHThxaYKnT3vJfJsSSlEjC2t5k1ekhDbv2Ja7iYkWtvguQTI-W8dqufKw7WtgM1bKN1OXUqP6VjeSZKaAJavuWt94uZEOanlTJVajemI0Lk8bvD3ZDQgyn6RT-xk89vS2H8oK0qXwWO78JKR6ybRdZxkqj2VUAUthmBDk6H2pcOUUD7R9Dn3no1nfiBmWDfEVZRveizgQ6iFepxptts6BA4UQFR47v6Haqz1M63jLDmFXcXbTcu-~N3B0HrsgdpiW-jF3d6unTxM~mtQMdrl3njf0DHNK48avBLhFzA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=98078456&ar=4083624&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig1.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">PG1115+080 as seen in <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (left) and Pan-STARRS (right). <em>Gaia</em>'s excellent resolution allows it to identify the three brightest quasar images, including both images in the merging pair, separated by 0.5 arcsec. <em>Gaia</em> detections are marked with white crosses.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a> of this paper, we outline the most important features of the <em>Gaia</em> satellite and how its source detection relates to quasars and lensed quasars. In Section 3, we consider this in the context of a sample of known lensed quasars with small image separations. In Sections\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> and 5, we present a technique to use <em>Gaia</em> to determine lens candidates and apply this technique to SDSS spectroscopically confirmed quasars, presenting potential small-separation lens candidates. We summarize our findings in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec6\"></span><a href=\"#sec6\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">6</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078458 id=\"98078458\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 GAIA</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em> is a space-based mission mapping the stars of the Milky Way with unprecedented astrometric precision. It is able to detect bright quasars, making it a useful tool for lensed quasar searches. In this section, we describe the basic <em>Gaia</em> mission and satellite details to explain the cataloguing of lensed quasars in <em>Gaia</em>'s first data release (hereafter GDR1).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078460 id=\"98078460\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1 Overview of the <em>Gaia</em> mission and satellite</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>Gaia</em> satellite (Gaia Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) was launched on 2013 December 19, with GDR1 consisting of observations from the first 14 months (2014 July 25 to 2015 September 16) of the nominal 60 month mission. <em>Gaia</em> consists of two identical telescopes, each with a rectangular aperture of 1.45 m\u00d70.45 m, that simultaneously point in directions separated by 106.5 degrees with beams folded to a common focal plane. As a result of the asymmetric focal plane with ratio 3:1, the PSF is also asymmetric with the same ratio and has a measured median FWHM of 103 mas (Fabricius et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) in the scanning axis direction. <em>Gaia</em> is located at L2 with a rotational sky-scanning orbit of period 6\u2009h and an orbital precession period of 63\u2009d. Over the course of <em>Gaia</em>'s five-year mission it will measure each source \u223c70\u00a0times.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078462 id=\"98078462\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-2>2.2 <em>Gaia</em> focal plane</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>Gaia</em> focal plane consists of 106 CCDs with 4500\u2009pixels in the along-scan (AL) direction and 1966\u2009pixels in the across-scan (AC) direction. Each pixel is rectangular in the ratio 1:3 similar to the PSF major and minor axes with size 10\u00d730 microns corresponding to 59\u00d7177 mas on the sky, i.e.\u00a0\u223c\u00a02\u2009pixels Nyquist sampling of the PSF FWHM. There are 14 sky-mapper (SM) CCDs and 62 astrometric field (AF) CCDs aligned in seven rows in the AL direction and nine columns in the AC direction with the middle CCD of the 9th AF column (AF9) assigned to one of the two focus wave front sensors (WFS). The SM CCDs are in two columns (SM1 and SM2) with baffling such that each SM can only view a single <em>Gaia</em> telescope whereas the AF CCDs view the two fields simultaneously. The integration time per CCD is 4.42 s but it is impossible to download all the pixels to Earth and hence astrometric measurements are made via windowed regions for sources detected in the SM CCDs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For sources brighter than G=13, 2D (AF) windows of 12 AC pixels (2.12 arcsec) and 18 AL pixels (1.06 arcsec) are transmitted to ground (Carrasco et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). For all fainter sources (G&gt;13) the windows are binned in the AC direction during readout to produce a 1D sample. For the faint sources brighter than G=16 (i.e. 13&lt;G&lt;16) there are 18 AL samples (Long 1D). For sources fainter than G=16 there are 12 AL samples (Short 1D) with total length of 0.71 arcsec. Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows the 12\u00d712\u2009pixel 2D window for a faint source, for which a 1D data stream of 12 samples is downloaded with a sampling resolution of 0.059 arcsec in the AL direction and 2.12 arcsec in the AC direction.</p>                        <a id=\"98078465\" scrollto-destination=\"98078465\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=p8tQCfclk10lvK97H2BtS1V2M~2BmisvPb~oL1mfjQkRL5~RGb9biAo2R8ffLHic7Q~WG3TKmIKTw3F~E2O5yVLgUt3JPMUI~peAikCNuA0Pxehs~ZOq8Q3LdVF6XS8sr9d9B6uN-fW8HV7DLngZ4getkGgwIwrMinyUPaZQP4-erLK0e51WAXwjXS-BG7mXu2DVXEcO2bCbhxSw1oFMHQ5q3xYScGKUTjPiaNJV18nTGKeA~PdEJ-to2nztwwxstdjpyVhJdLtXqLC91ZeAojABzDGVw2XlErtJL6Og45guFE8UHrRhXZZUlur3aGVW5efwXOGAE94pJD7dqTiOIw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A schematic of the sampling of a faint (G&gt;16) source in a Gaia astrometric field CCD. The ellipses represent the images of point sources on the focal plane.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=p8tQCfclk10lvK97H2BtS1V2M~2BmisvPb~oL1mfjQkRL5~RGb9biAo2R8ffLHic7Q~WG3TKmIKTw3F~E2O5yVLgUt3JPMUI~peAikCNuA0Pxehs~ZOq8Q3LdVF6XS8sr9d9B6uN-fW8HV7DLngZ4getkGgwIwrMinyUPaZQP4-erLK0e51WAXwjXS-BG7mXu2DVXEcO2bCbhxSw1oFMHQ5q3xYScGKUTjPiaNJV18nTGKeA~PdEJ-to2nztwwxstdjpyVhJdLtXqLC91ZeAojABzDGVw2XlErtJL6Og45guFE8UHrRhXZZUlur3aGVW5efwXOGAE94pJD7dqTiOIw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A schematic of the sampling of a faint (G&gt;16) source in a Gaia astrometric field CCD. The ellipses represent the images of point sources on the focal plane.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig2.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/98078465/stx2094fig2.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig2.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"98078465\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/stx2094fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&Signature=xfhrPR0f8UDJLgjQrBSmoaee~BO3A4J6vwoU3RtqxOP1lIAFTMWS5hnC35f0JBoayoCG0wgKQWgzU8gxEbkZqe6TjHP~XlglyiY-8d2qZlG7q2S1aTSIPpghbQukNVuEf1sh7mP80Y4HnE8-y-bxlYdx171ItLQpOrV7DUt8k-gw1XDecp3e6cPT2aYZISknP4m71AHXiaxPjqP4xGFiM0lqjxlwYNzThClkK7N3o01eZfU~xZcrbmy0BonRD5DKY1CcCHF7KN2Ow2CVQRX16RsHW00FGFl~b~6O9YNdW-83XciVh7nPyD4DyowWDlIsQUN5PTkEkIF26vlRY4LacQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=98078465&ar=4083624&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig2.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A schematic of the sampling of a faint (G&gt;16) source in a <em>Gaia</em> astrometric field CCD. The ellipses represent the images of point sources on the focal plane.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In principle, each component of a faint binary with separation greater than 2.12 arcsec will always be isolated in their window. Since each source will be observed multiple times with different scan directions during the five-year <em>Gaia</em> mission, the 1D data can be used to create a 2D reconstruction (Harrison\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). At the current time the individual 1D scans have not been released by the <em>Gaia</em> project.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078467 id=\"98078467\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-3>2.3 Catalogue creation</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Source measurements are based on modelling single point sources in these 2D windowed regions of pixels or 1D samples. When close pairs are encountered in the same windowed region, depending on the scanning direction and relative orientation of the pair, the fainter object is given a truncated window of pixels, which has not been processed for GDR1 (Fabricius et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). Therefore there is a significant lack of detections of the fainter companion in close binaries in GDR1.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The main astrometric source catalogue for GDR1 contains over one billion sources (see Lindegren et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a> for details). The fluxes are measured in a wide optical band (hereafter <em>G</em> band) as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. 37 million sources were removed before GDR1 was published, when they were separated from another source by less than five times the combined astrometric positional uncertainty. This was due to the cataloguing of known objects against an initial <em>Gaia</em> source list, which catalogued many objects twice. Further objects were filtered from GDR1 if sources were observed fewer than five times (five focal plane transits), or if their astrometric excess noise and positional standard uncertainty were greater than 20 and 100 mas, respectively. Finally, sources were removed if they had fewer than 11 <em>G</em>-band measurements (CCD transits in the astrometric part of the focal plane). See Fabricius et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) for a full explanation of the data-processing and catalogue creation. The limiting magnitude of <em>Gaia</em> is \u223c20.7 in the Vega magnitude system.</p>                        <a id=\"98078470\" scrollto-destination=\"98078470\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/stx2094fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&Signature=jzo7pQu7CeH-au2e3yF6hyDOwKuViETiSK7toEWadV72Dy13DyohvDqJ1xZCTYD2bUEyhEoDMXLHHXOd5uieMxuaZPaenMCWJNORFpKsd~qy7DtHMJcEZNvSZ0mslQe2bKeRbbkwdlTsZxNeBhvOiiBLUmVnhnpFgXNJkkg1zNQd0wbWmGGWGokcVeVTf3kzkVKnOVoiISA7qmat0oPRYAgF24sbXpbx6mWmh4KWZ671jM5OB-F~gEa13Av2DnxlmT66KTZWJmcr8nmYA4ZoHtGHfmwG4g4CjE07J0RveeJtPhU-i9pW5BjvgGetgMg4dRTBR~83gWzcz6LK9oQj6w__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=98078470&ar=4083624&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The nominal <em>Gaia</em><em>G</em> band in black, with SDSS <em>ugriz</em> filters overlaid.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078471 id=\"98078471\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-4>2.4 Close pairs in <em>Gaia</em></h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Where <em>Gaia</em> can provide the most use for lensed quasar searches is in determining whether a source is composed of multiple stellar objects. In ground-based optical imaging surveys, the typically much larger FWHM causes many contaminant systems to resemble lensed quasars. These contaminants include single quasars with bright host galaxies, quasars or stars blended with galaxies, projected systems and starburst galaxies with quasar-like colours. Because of the truncated windows given to fainter companions around brighter neighbours by <em>Gaia</em>, there is a limitation in <em>Gaia</em>'s handling of close pairs (Arenou et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We demonstrate how this issue affects lensed quasars in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>. The blue histogram shows the distribution of separations of detected <em>Gaia</em> pairs in a sparse field out of the galactic plane (l\u00a0\u223c\u00a0173\u00b0, b\u00a0\u223c\u00a067\u00b0), i.e. a field in which <em>Gaia</em> is able to read out all objects within the magnitude threshold. The green line is the expected distribution from randomly positioned sources on the sky matched to a large-separation asymptote. We expect an overdensity of close-separation objects relative to this line associated with stellar binaries, bright star-forming mergers and even lensed quasars, however the <em>Gaia</em> catalogue significantly lacks the detection of the second object below 2.5 arcsec. The pairs with very small <em>Gaia</em> separations are possibly duplicate sources that could not be filtered from GDR1 (Arenou et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). We also show the numbers of lensed quasars <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">OM10</a> predicted across the whole sky with either the brightest object detectable by <em>Gaia</em> (approximately equal to an <em>i</em>-band magnitude\u00a0&lt;\u00a020.7) or with at least two images detectable by <em>Gaia</em>, in bins of 0.1 arcsec separation. The <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">OM10</a> catalogue is truncated at an image separation of 0.5 arcsec. This cut was made due to difficulty in detection and characterization of lenses at lower image separations, however <em>Gaia</em> will likely be able to push past this limit in detection. Finet &amp; Surdej (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) discuss the detection of smaller image separation lensed quasars in <em>Gaia</em>, however in this paper, we use the full <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">OM10</a> catalogue for predicted numbers of lenses since we are interested in those that can be characterized in ground-based imaging data. The area under the magenta curve of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> shows that later <em>Gaia</em> catalogues, which will include secondary source detections in close binaries, should detect the multiple images of\u00a0\u223c900 lensed quasars, including\u00a0\u223c240 quadruply-imaged systems. This value is in agreement with other estimates based on <em>Gaia</em>'s expected pre-launch capabilities (Surdej et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>; Finet &amp; Surdej\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). The number of lensed quasars expected with only one image detected by <em>Gaia</em> is \u223c1400, only \u223c50 of which are quads. This very low quad fraction is due to the brightest two images of quads often having similarly magnified fluxes.</p>                        <a id=\"98078473\" scrollto-destination=\"98078473\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=cPYaH4WUeihvhq87SSxweWpOgZBvTDa~CF~CqinNoME82HIk6JZeE0M25p263p5hMIV2zi-u8OV19BHxDUA5IZ2bNAqXDN3LQ2KVonGfj7xRA8rB85fqQMxFOPpFg15tIEjlEcKfA-DCpUeW4HfqSCm8OfdT3C5ujDqU2zoYgH5YaX31x2skY0aL8X0p-0BLlYGEFQTmp9crnzhDJv3Feex3dsngSbWZdI3xpl3QwpE3YNnEvSXfOqT~Fx5gbGC51cWAbBUYYLbd1k39jOte-DEMB32lR5W4WWVGISZJWCmenrKu5lmfNK0peScVzuOCrQQRFi8ls74IPTHFaANfIA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Expected numbers of lensed quasars as a function of image separation in 0.1 arcsec bins. The green line is the distribution expected from a field of randomly-positioned sources.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078474 id=\"98078474\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 A SAMPLE OF SMALL-SEPARATION LENSED QUASARS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">At separations larger than 2 arcsec, the images of lensed quasars are deblended in ground-based surveys and <em>Gaia</em> cannot provide much further information, though it is still useful in some cases, for example in detecting quasar images around bright lensing galaxies, where the system might remain blended.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To investigate how <em>Gaia</em> catalogues known close-separation lensed quasars, we compile a set of 49 lensed quasars in the SDSS footprint with image separations less than 2 arcsec and which are typically blended in SDSS. All lenses in the sample have at least one <em>Gaia</em> detection, but only eight have multiple detections in GDR1, whereas 43 are expected to have further images brighter than the detection limit. Only one lens, PG1115+080, is detected as three separate images (though we note this is one of the larger-separation systems) and is deblended into two components in SDSS. These eight systems are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> with <em>Gaia</em> detections overlaid on SDSS <em>gri</em> colour images.</p>                        <a id=\"98078477\" scrollto-destination=\"98078477\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=ZZTBAUQF4VIo1Eh3NHg4eozi35cg7OZZqGRI5QGCCYJllpA8oSgbepU2xAPv5518T8jgWDTZgmZj8BUD5vFOKwS2nSedtIBNrlGmukaCrX5Pr2zn5zbb08aFuChIcMexoKWLkkkErhfi7Okw68lt0lTun4PeAhrO1KMl7om7Px~LDepEdwRfz96gBHWAJfwklgBMUkqxJ7TJ1mnMp6vq1l2~0OnfClrdCWI8JOfEph1qS8SdmdCACgZL-j1q27b~by6laJT4jUoB~9zaS1mbpb57aoazefbXKRx9kFIvMql8wZg89s8Ak~ZEtna6dWkLbueyHlmi4~VwAXfRnYSJeg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The eight small-separation lensed quasars that have multiple detections in the Gaia catalogue with positions overlaid on an SDSS gri colour image. These lenses are: SDSSJ0746+4403 (Inada et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), HS0810+2554 (Reimers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>), SBS0909+532 (Kochanek et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1997</a>), PG1115+080 (Weymann et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1980</a>), SDSSJ1258+1657 (Inada et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>), SDSSJ1332+0347 (Morokuma et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), SDSSJ1334+3315 (Rusu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) and SDSSJ1406+6126 (Inada et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>).</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Since many objects were removed as possible duplicates in GDR1, we check for duplicate removal of the non-catalogued images through the <em>duplicated_source</em> flag. Approximately 5 percent of objects in GDR1 have this flag. For the lensed quasars with single <em>Gaia</em> detections where multiple detections are expected, only 4 of 35 are flagged as duplicated sources, indicating the further missing detection(s) in <em>Gaia</em> may be due to truncated windows being given to the missing images in certain scan directions. Since these windows have not yet been processed, not all quasar images have enough <em>G</em>-band measurements to be included in GDR1 (Fabricius et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=98078479 id=\"98078479\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 FINDING QUASAR LENSES IN <em>GAIA</em></h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Since small-separation lenses are much more common than the easily deblended larger-separation lenses (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>), we now explore several ways that GDR1 can help identify these small-separation lensed quasars by considering our sample of known lenses. First, we consider multiple <em>Gaia</em> detections corresponding to the multiple images of a lensed quasar. In the case of single <em>Gaia</em> detections, we investigate the <em>Gaia</em> catalogue parameter of astrometric excess noise which might hint at the presence of a lensing galaxy or further quasar images. Finally, we compare <em>Gaia</em> fluxes and positions to other data sets that blend lensed quasars into single objects, which will naturally measure larger fluxes and different centroids from those given in the <em>Gaia</em> catalogue.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Throughout the rest of this paper we use spectroscopically confirmed objects from the <em>SpecObjAll</em> table from the twelfth data release of SDSS (Alam et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), in which most spectroscopically confirmed quasars are from SDSS-III BOSS, but also includes spectra from all previous SDSS data releases.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078482 id=\"98078482\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-1>4.1 Multiple <em>Gaia</em> detections</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Our sample of small-separation lensed quasars shows that several objects are still separated into multiple components by <em>Gaia</em>. Arenou et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) have shown that the detection of both components of close pairs is more likely when they are of similar magnitude since the primary object can be either of the pair in different focal plane transits, leading to both objects being catalogued in GDR1. We see this in the eight lensed quasars with multiple detections in our sample (see Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). Therefore even in GDR1 it is possible to use <em>Gaia</em>'s resolution to deblend potential quasar lens candidates into multiple objects immediately. A common limitation to finding lensed quasars in ground-based imaging surveys is the lensing galaxy causing a possible contamination to the colour of the object, meaning conventional quasar colour classification will miss these objects (see Ostrovski et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a> for a full discussion). However, given the prior that two point sources must be present means <em>Gaia</em> can help detect these bright lens galaxy systems, relying less on constraints from quasar colour-selection techniques.</p>                        <a id=\"98078484\" scrollto-destination=\"98078484\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl1\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table 1.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Details of small-separation lensed quasars with multiple <em>Gaia</em> detections (source redshift, image separation and <em>Gaia</em> magnitudes). <sup><em>a</em></sup>Values for PG1115+080 are for the close-separation merging pair.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th>Name<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>z</em><span class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/98078484\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=98078485 id=\"98078485\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2>4.2 Single <em>Gaia</em> detections</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">As discussed in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2-4\"></span><a href=\"#sec2-4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2.4</a>, most bright lensed quasars will have one detection in GDR1. However, even when <em>Gaia</em> detects a single image of a lensed quasar, it provides useful information in its catalogue values of astrometric excess noise, position and flux for each detection. The latter two measurements are useful because we are able to search for systems with missing flux and/or a different centroid relative to that of a data set capturing all the flux, e.g. from proper imaging, where the difference would be caused by <em>Gaia</em> not cataloguing all sources. That is, GDR1 effectively resolves out the flux from single images of lensed quasars. For a given object, a potential close-separation quasar lens or perhaps just a single quasar, we outline how to derive a synthesized <em>Gaia</em> magnitude from ground-based broad-band survey photometry, with which one can compare the <em>Gaia</em> magnitude. In the case of a large discrepancy, this could be accounted for by the presence of extra quasar images and a lensing galaxy, which the ground-based imaging has blended with the original <em>Gaia</em> detection. To determine the centroid difference we use SDSS positions, which have been recalibrated as in Deason et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). The detection of only one image of a lensed quasar in <em>Gaia</em> will not be limited to the first <em>Gaia</em> data release. Indeed \u223c1400 lenses (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2-4\"></span><a href=\"#sec2-4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2.4</a>) will have just one component bright enough to be detected by <em>Gaia</em> but have other images fainter than the magnitude threshold, as can be seen by the difference between the two lensed quasar population histograms in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=98078487 id=\"98078487\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2-1>4.2.1 Astrometric Excess Noise</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We initially consider the catalogue value of <em>astrometric excess noise</em> (hereafter AEN). This parameter represents the scatter in the astrometric model for a single object (Lindegren et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). A large AEN might indicate the presence of uncatalogued, nearby images in lenses or perhaps a bright lensing galaxy. We match <em>Gaia</em> observations to the SDSS spectroscopic catalogues for stars, galaxies and quasars within 0.5 arcsec. We further require no flags from the spectral classification and recover 534\u2009172 stars, 286\u2009488 galaxies and 218\u2009980 quasars. We plot their magnitude and AEN distributions in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, splitting the quasar sample into those above and below redshift 0.3. This plot reflects the method to separate galaxies from stars using AEN, as used in Belokurov et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) and Koposov, Belokurov &amp; Torrealba (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). From the overlaid positions corresponding to lensed quasars, AEN alone cannot sufficiently identify most lensed quasars, however it is a useful parameter to consider for the brightest candidates. Therefore we turn our attention to using other data sets and <em>Gaia</em> catalogue parameters to indicate missing components from the catalogue.</p>                        <a id=\"98078489\"                  <h4 scrollto-destination=98078490 id=\"98078490\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2-2>4.2.2 Flux deficit</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em>'s 0.1 arcsec PSF FWHM means that it can completely resolve the images of any useful lensed quasar (those that have large enough image separation to be spectroscopically confirmed). Therefore the <em>Gaia</em> flux measurement is truly an indication of the magnitude of a single image of the system. Comparing this value to a ground-based flux measurement of the entire blended system may allow the detection of the presence of further components in the system. First, we must determine a relationship between the <em>Gaia</em><em>G</em>-band and ground-based photometric magnitudes.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To derive an empirical <em>G</em>-band fit for quasars, we need an isolated sample of quasars with <em>Gaia</em> detections. We use the extensive spectroscopically confirmed quasar catalogues from SDSS, which we match to the <em>Gaia</em> secondary source catalogue. On attempting to fit an empirical relation between the SDSS <em>ugriz</em> magnitudes and <em>Gaia</em>, the variability over the \u223c10 yr mean epoch difference is apparent (2015 for <em>Gaia</em> and 2003 for SDSS). Therefore we use the much better matched mean epoch of Pan-STARRS (Chambers et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), 2013.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For the photometric fit we use the Pan-STARRS PSF magnitudes to ensure that we are comparing to the flux from just the quasar, since this is what <em>Gaia</em> is measuring. We apply the following cuts to the combined spectroscopic and photometric catalogues: <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">spectroscopic class = QSO</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a05</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">zerr\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.05</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">zwarning = 0</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">no <em>Gaia</em>, SDSS or Pan-STARRS neighbours within 5 arcsec</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">type = 6 (PSF morphology in SDSS)</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">Pan-STARRS <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>kron</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.05</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em>-SDSS centroid distance &lt;0.1\u2009arcsec</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>Gaia</em>-SDSS proper motion\u00a0&lt;\u00a05 mas yr<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></li></ul></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Though the SDSS PSF morphology removes the majority of objects with bright hosts or nearby neighbours, we also apply an <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>kron</sub> magnitude cut to the deeper Pan-STARRS data to remove further extended objects. Finally, once an empirical fit to the <em>G</em>-band photometry is made, we remove those with the most inconsistent flux when compared to <em>Gaia</em> (5\u03c3 outliers in a given magnitude bin) and repeat the fit. We are left with a catalogue of 117\u2009599 <em>Gaia</em>-matched isolated quasars.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">We are now able to fit an empirical <em>G</em>-band magnitude from the Pan-STARRS photometry, via a simple linear combination of <em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, <em>i</em> and <em>z</em>: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ1\" content-id=\"equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n {G_{{\\rm synth}}} = \\alpha + {r} + \\beta ({g-r}) + \\gamma ({r-i}) + \\delta ({i-z})\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\sigma }^{2} = {\\sigma _{{\\rm int}}}^{2} + {\\sigma _{{\\rm G}}}^{2} + {\\sigma _{{\\rm {\\it G}synth}}}^{2}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ3\" content-id=\"equ3\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n {{\\rm log}} P = -\\frac{\\left({G-G_{{\\rm synth}}}\\right)^{2}}{2 {\\sigma }^{2}}-\\frac{\\rm {log}(2 \\pi {\\sigma }^{2})}{2}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(3)</span></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">This is well motivated since the nominal <em>Gaia</em> bandpass roughly overlaps these optical filters, as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. To include the high-redshift quasars in the sample we allow for their <em>g</em>-band dropout by fitting for two separate formulae at some <em>g</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>r</em> cut. We optimise using the log likelihood of equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ3\"></span><a href=\"#equ3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>), determining the parameters \u03b1, \u03b2, \u03b3, \u03b4, \u03c3<sub>int</sub> and the <em>g</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>r</em> cut-off.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">On applying this method to SDSS DR9 photometry we find an intrinsic dispersion of 0.263\u2009mag. The fit to Pan-STARRS gives an improved intrinsic dispersion of 0.151\u2009mag, reflecting the increased brightness variation of quasars over larger time-scales. The best-fitting formulae using Pan-STARRS photometry<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> are: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ4\" content-id=\"equ4\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n&amp;&amp;{ {g-r} &lt; 0.11:} \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp;&amp; {G} \\!=\\! -0.033 \\!+\\! {r} \\!+\\! 0.131 ({g-r}) \\!-\\! 0.660 ({r-i}) \\!-\\! 0.162 ({i-z})\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(4)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ5\" content-id=\"equ5\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n&amp;&amp;{ {g-r} &gt; 0.11:} \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp;&amp; {G} \\!=\\! -0.061 \\!+\\! {r} \\!+\\! 0.217 ({g-r}) \\!-\\! 0.548 ({r-i}) \\!-\\! 0.013 ({i-z}).\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(5)</span></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a> shows the residual magnitudes against redshift. The non-uniform redshift distribution of the quasar sample does not significantly bias the fit given the very similar dispersion at all redshifts. Furthermore, the residuals are essentially Gaussian-distributed about zero (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a>). Estimates of median variability for this sample between <em>Gaia</em> and Pan-STARRS or SDSS are \u223c0.1 or \u223c0.15, respectively (Hook et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1994</a>; MacLeod et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Our dispersions are larger than these estimates because of the error from the simple model fit to all quasar spectral shapes and redshifts.</p>                        <a id=\"98078499\" scrollto-destination=\"98078499\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig7\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 7.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/m_stx2094fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&amp;Signature=Wj9x5g~2a6u4737F090jrZtNmrigGS4gQcryO7Zp3lqT7GjVl2--aQCRyVTINriTX7Y-PwlLbSz53dvrmX2Z1v4IAGmNohVsLAaSOzVYmCfQ6gQpEAaM3cUBlZANnS8cjvGZipEsFNeCR3hBAy3kVJo7hlgAIVI~r5uU83lu1veYLIvgFKpMStIhgo2SgiZqaHMu0r1FNcQLWRWt4Ipv5NBFxD6bOG2rvi7NchGa1oX1Me9t0Jcj7WjKS4G1F0dusZyC-ibpkR8-Wr5-e1c~A8T6bv8FSaW90HXTZ~FEJJ3QWel3dv4Wo706A~Ejsx0N5VXeEgiNXk5M1b4X1GcE~Q__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The top plot shows synthesized GaiaG-band residuals against redshift for our sample of 117\u2009599 isolated quasars. The bottom plot compares the fit to SDSS and Pan-STARRS data and equivalent Gaussians from the median absolute deviation are overlaid.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig7.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/98078499/stx2094fig7.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig7.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"98078499\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/472/4/10.1093_mnras_stx2094/1/stx2094fig7.jpeg?Expires=1611466491&Signature=Bu0TcnotY~R6K9oywu~Ck~hz-5p8BaAPpSwYL3ViGG3zVysFg2GhQXiAQBx6tXliVMvaaduAPQOGFXmx~hOzPqSJMG~zgbRzTYJkIm0vI5I8aOqqumu3zEIMrLNmF7x8SwCQl1TJMcdmlfMo2vCDPxfKBreOLDe0~vMNjzr5ORRZIp-e4NOfWs3MEZOVINL5w5jIInkZt9Pu4ifWV5yhFXr4ulPfnAZOMy6kQQF6BC4Ps3QFjmfB3GgRxSxtyHaRSYtotk40rp8fZEXgnCYtYcs-M5Ak6MbVYnQq9bsQq2ifq0QMaPHjYnKsIJ5sFvXLkOMASYcfOI5FxcVDoWS7tw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=98078499&ar=4083624&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stx2094fig7.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The top plot shows synthesized <em>Gaia</em><em>G</em>-band residuals against redshift for our sample of 117\u2009599 isolated quasars. The overdensities are due to the non-uniform redshift distribution. The median and median absolute deviations are plotted at each redshift (black solid and dotted lines, respectively). The bottom plot compares the fit to SDSS and Pan-STARRS data and equivalent Gaussians from the median absolute deviation are overlaid.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We apply these empirical <em>G</em>-band fits to the sample of lensed quasars and compare them to <em>Gaia</em> measurements in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=98078501 id=\"98078501\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2-3>4.2.3 Positional Offsets</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Quasar lenses are often discovered by looking for extended quasar candidates. This is done by performing a cut in a PSF magnitude minus a model magnitude (fit to a galaxy profile), where it is expected that a PSF magnitude will not capture all the flux of an extended object. However, this technique may fail to find all lensed quasars, especially those with small separations. To determine the limitations of this method, we match all <em>Gaia</em> pairs (two catalogue detections) between 0.5 and 1.0 arcsec separation to SDSS and compute <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub> (in this case we use the SDSS PSF and CMODEL magnitudes in the <em>r</em> band; Stoughton et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>). We keep all pairs with brightness differences within 1 mag (based on their <em>Gaia</em><em>G</em>-band magnitudes) as is typical for lensed quasar image fluxes, and remove objects obviously made up from non-point sources. We find a spread in <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub> of 0.076\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.049 for objects with image separation 0.5\u20130.6 arcsec and 0.215\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.117 for those with image separation 0.9\u20131.0 arcsec. To understand these values we compare to single PSF objects (isolated <em>Gaia</em> detections) and find a value of 0.003\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.042. Therefore, given the overlap in the <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub> values for single stars and pairs at separations of 0.5 arcsec, the <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub> magnitude comparison for classification of extended/point source objects will often not be able to distinguish between relatively close binaries and single stellar objects. Furthermore, the sample of pairs we have used here is highly biased to objects of similar magnitude (as explained in Arenou et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a> and due to our magnitude difference cut) whereas objects with large flux ratios will tend to appear even more PSF-like, implying a conservative upper bound on our <em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub> values for pairs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Given this discussion, it is clear that the PSF classification of SDSS (<em>r</em><sub>PSF</sub>\u2212<em>r</em><sub>model</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.145) will class many binaries and small-separation lensed quasars as PSFs. This is perhaps less likely for quad lensed quasars in which there are four images; however, the image separations of the brightest components can often be much smaller than the Einstein radius, leading to a more PSF-like appearance.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Therefore we again turn to <em>Gaia</em>'s excellent resolution to define a parameter indicative of multiple system components. When a system is composed of two close stellar components, <em>Gaia</em> will catalogue the centroid of one of the two components with high precision and accuracy. However, the same system in ground-based imaging will be blended and the catalogued centroid will lie between the two objects, offset from the <em>Gaia</em> centroid. This offset can easily be calculated and should be large for crowded systems like lensed quasars. Jackson &amp; Browne (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>) suggest searching for an offset between optical and radio positions for identifying lensed quasars in radio surveys. Our method is similar however we rely on both the galaxy and uncatalogued quasar images to cause the offset and only require optical data. While this offset might not be significant for doubles with large flux ratios, we always expect it to be apparent for quadruply-lensed quasars, since flux ratios between the two brightest images are approximately unity.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">To robustly compare the difference in <em>Gaia</em> and SDSS positions, we must understand the minimum uncertainty expected from fitting a single PSF. Using a Gaussian PSF defined only by the FWHM and assuming at least critical sampling, any unbiased estimator is limited to a centroid positional error of\u00a0\u223c\u00a0(FWHM)/(signal to noise) (Mendez, Silva &amp; Lobos\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). The FWHM, signal and noise are inferred from the SDSS catalogues in the <em>r</em> band since the standard SDSS positions are derived from the <em>r</em> band. In order to look for sub-pixel offsets we must have very accurate SDSS astrometry. We therefore use the <em>Gaia</em>-based calibration of SDSS positions explained in Deason et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Using this catalogue, the median <em>Gaia</em>-SDSS offset for quasars brighter than 19th magnitude is 0.02 arcsec. We use these recalibrated SDSS positions to define an offset parameter (OP): <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ6\" content-id=\"equ6\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\rm OP} = \\frac{{\\rm distance\\, between\\, {\\it Gaia}\\, and\\, SDSS\\, centroids}}{{\\rm SDSS\\, PSF\\, centroid\\, uncertainty}}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(6)</span></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The spread of this offset parameter in combination with the flux deficit from Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4-2-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec4-2-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4.2.2</a> can be seen for lenses and SDSS spectroscopically confirmed quasars in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig8\" data-open=\"fig8\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">8</a> (see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec5-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">5.2</a> for details).</p>                        <a id=\"98078507\" scrollto-destination=\"98078507\"></a>\r\n<div
